<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See statutes cited in n.&nbsp;20, <em>supra</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>He might, of course, have thought delusively he was doing something just as wrongful as the act charged against him, but this is not the test: he must have understood that he was committing the act charged and that it was wrongful, see Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. 13-502(A)  (West 2001) ("A person may be found guilty except insane if at the time of the commission of the criminal act the person was afflicted with a mental disease or defect of such severity that the person did not know the criminal act was wrong").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We think this logic holds true in the face of the usual rule of statutory construction of "&nbsp;'&nbsp;"giv[ing] effect, if possible, to every clause and word of a statute,"&nbsp;'&nbsp;" <em>Duncan</em> v. <em>Walker, </em>533 U.&nbsp;S. 167, 174 (2001) (quoting <em>United States</em> v. <em>Menasche,</em> 348 U.&nbsp;S. 528, 538-539 (1955)); see also 2 J. Sutherland, Statutes and Statutory Construction 4705 (3d ed. 1943).  Insanity standards are formulated to guide the factfinder to determine the blameworthiness of a mentally ill defendant.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Jones</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 463 U.&nbsp;S. 354, 373, n. 4 (1983) (Brennan, J., dissenting).  The <em>M'Naghten</em> test is a sequential test, first asking the factfinder to conduct the easier enquiry whether a defendant knew the nature and quality of his actions.  If not, the defendant is to be considered insane and there is no need to pass to the harder and broader enquiry whether the defendant knew his actions were wrong.  And, because, owing to this sequence, the factfinder is to ask whether a defendant lacks moral capacity only when he possesses cognitive capacity, the only defendants who will be found to lack moral capacity are those possessing cognitive capacity.  Cf. 2 C. Torcia, Wharton's Criminal Law 101 (15th ed. 1994).  Though, before 1993, Arizona had in place the full <em>M'Naghten </em>test with this sequential enquiry, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Schantz</em>, 98 Ariz., at 207, 403 P.&nbsp;2d, at 525, it would appear that the legislature eliminated the cognitive capacity part not to change the meaning of the insanity standard but to implement its judgment that a streamlined standard with only the moral capacity part would be easier for the jury to apply, see Arizona House of Representative Judiciary Committee Notes 3 (Mar. 18, 1993); 1 R. Gerber, Criminal Law of Arizona 502-6, 502-11 (2d ed. 1993 and Supp. 2000).  This is corroborated by the State's choice for many years against revising the applicable recommended jury instruction (enumerating the complete <em>M'Naghten </em>test) in order to match the amended statutory standard.  See 1 Gerber, <em>supra</em>, at 502-6.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We thus think the dissent reads <em>Mott </em>too broadly.  See <em>post</em>, at 6-7 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.) (no distinction between observation and mental-disease testimony, see <em>infra</em>, at 16-17, or lay and expert).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The more natural reading of <em>Mott</em> suggests to us that this evidence cannot be considered as to <em>mens rea</em> even if the defendant establishes his insanity, though one might read <em>Mott </em>otherwise.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Clark's parents testified that, in the months before the shooting and even days beforehand, Clark called them "aliens" and thought that "aliens" were out to get him.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Tr. of Bench Trial in No. CR 2000-538, pp. 110-112, 136, 226-228 (Aug. 20, 2003).  One night before the shooting, according to Clark's mother, Clark repeatedly viewed a popular film characterized by her as telling a story about "aliens" masquerading as government agents, a story Clark insisted was real despite his mother's protestations to the contrary.  See <em>id.</em>, at 59-60 (Aug. 21, 2003).  And two months after the shooting, Clark purportedly told his parents that his hometown, Flagstaff, was inhabited principally by "aliens," who had to be stopped, and that the only way to stop them was with bullets.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>id.</em>, at 131-132 (Aug. 20, 2003); <em>id.</em>, at 24-25 (Aug. 21, 2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This is contrary to the dissent's understanding.  See <em>post</em>, at 2-3 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In conflict with the dissent's characterization, see <em>post</em>, at 2 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.), it does not always, however, come from experts.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>Arizona permits capacity evidence, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>State </em>v. <em>Sanchez</em>, 117 Ariz. 369, 373, 573 P.&nbsp;2d 60, 64 (1977); see also Ariz. Rule Evid. 704 (2006) (allowing otherwise admissible evidence on testimony "embrac[ing] an ultimate issue to be decided by the trier of fact"), though not every jurisdiction permits such evidence on the ultimate issue of insanity.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Fed. Rule Evid. 704(b) ("No expert witness testifying with respect to the mental state or condition of a defendant in a criminal case may state an opinion or inference as to whether the defendant did or did not have the mental state or condition constituting an element of the crime charged or a defense thereto.  Such ultimate issues are matters for the trier of fact alone"); <em>United States </em>v. <em>Dixon</em>, 185 F.&nbsp;3d 393, 400 (CA5 1999) (in the face of mental-disease evidence, Rule 704(b) prohibits an expert "from testifying that [the mental-disease evidence] does or does not prevent the defendant from appreciating the wrongfulness of his actions").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Arizona permits evidence bearing on insanity to be presented by either lay or expert witnesses.  See <em>State </em>v. <em>Bay</em>, 150 Ariz. 112, 116, 722 P.&nbsp;2d 280, 284 (1986).  According to <em>Bay</em>, "[f]oundationally, a lay witness must have had an opportunity to observe the past conduct and history of a defendant; the fact that he is a lay witness goes not to the admissibility of the testimony but rather to its weight."  <em>Ibid.</em> (citation omitted); see also <em>State </em>v. <em>Hughes</em>, 193 Ariz. 72, 83, 969 P.&nbsp;2d 1184, 1195 (1998).  In fact, a defendant can theoretically establish insanity solely via lay testimony.  See <em>Bay</em>, 150 Ariz., at 116, 722 P.&nbsp;2d, at 284.  But cf. <em>State </em>v. <em>McMurtrey</em>, 136 Ariz. 93, 100, 664 P.&nbsp;2d 637, 644 (1983) ("[I]t is difficult to imagine how a defendant could place his or her sanity in issue ... without expert testimony as to the defendant's state of mind at the time of the crime").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNopinion1.32" href="#FRopinion1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;With respect to "the limited factual issues the trial court held it could consider under [Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. ]13-502 and <em>Mott</em>, defense counsel made no additional 'offer of proof' at the conclusion of the case but preserved [Clark's] legal contentions by asking the court to consider all of the evidence presented in determining whether the state had proved its case."  Brief for Petitioner 10, n.&nbsp;20 (citations omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNopinion1.33" href="#FRopinion1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We do not agree with the State's argument that the failure to make an offer of proof, see n. 4, <em>supra</em>, is a bar to pressing Clark's claim about the admissibility of mental-illness or capacity evidence as to <em>mens rea</em>, see Brief for Respondent 27-29, especially when the Arizona Court of Appeals rejected Clark's argument on the merits rather than clearly on this ground, see App. 351-353; see also <em>Michigan</em> v. <em>Long</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 1032, 1042 (1983) ("[I]t is not clear from the opinion itself that the state court relied upon an adequate and independent state ground and ... it fairly appears that the state court rested its decision primarily on federal law").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNopinion1.34" href="#FRopinion1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>We therefore have no reason to believe that the courts of Arizona would have failed to restrict their application of <em>Mott </em>to the professional testimony the <em>Mott </em>opinion was stated to cover, if Clark's counsel had specified any observation evidence he claimed to be generally admissible and relevant to <em>mens rea</em>.  Nothing that we hold here is authority for restricting a factfinder's consideration of observation evidence indicating state of mind at the time of a criminal offense (conventional <em>mens rea </em>evidence) as distinct from professional mental-disease or capacity evidence going to ability to form a certain state of mind during a period that includes the time of the offense charged.  And, of course, nothing held here prevents Clark from raising this discrete claim when the case returns to the courts of Arizona, if consistent with the State's procedural rules.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A legislature is nonetheless free to require affirmative proof of sanity by the way it describes a criminal offense, see <em>Dixon</em> v. <em>United States</em>, <em>ante</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 7-9).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although a desired evidentiary use is restricted, that is not equivalent to a <em>Sandstrom</em> presumption.  See <em>Sandstrom</em> v. <em>Montana,</em> 442 U.&nbsp;S. 510, 514-524 (1979) (due process forbids use of presumption that relieves the prosecution of burden of proving mental state by inference of intent from an act).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p> In fact, Oregon had this scheme in place when we decided <em>Leland</em> v. <em>Oregon</em>, 343 U.&nbsp;S. 790, 794-796 (1952).  We do not, however, read any part of <em>Leland</em> to require as a matter of due process that evidence of incapacity be considered to rebut the <em>mens rea</em> element of a crime.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>We reject the State's argument that <em>mens rea</em> and insanity, as currently understood, are entirely distinguishable, so that mental-disease and capacity evidence relevant to insanity is simply irrelevant to <em>mens rea</em>.  Not only does evidence accepted as showing insanity trump <em>mens rea</em>, but evidence of behavior close to the time of the act charged may indicate both the actual state of mind at that time and also an enduring incapacity to form the criminal state of mind necessary to the offense charged.  See Brief for American Psychiatric Association et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 12-13; Arenella, The Diminished Capacity and Diminished Responsibility Defenses: Two Children of a Doomed Marriage, 77 Colum. L.&nbsp;Rev. 827, 834-835 (1977); cf. <em>Powell</em> v. <em>Texas,</em> 392 U.&nbsp;S. 514, 535-536 (1968) (plurality opinion) (the "doctrines of <em>actus reus</em>, <em>mens rea</em>, insanity, mistake, justification, and duress" are a "collection of interlocking and overlapping concepts which the common law has utilized to assess the moral accountability of an individual for his antisocial deeds").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Clark's argument assumes that Arizona's rule is a rule of evidence, rather than a redefinition of <em>mens rea</em>, see <em>Montana</em> v. <em>Egelhoff,</em> 518 U.&nbsp;S. 37, 58-59 (1996) (<em>Ginsburg, J.</em>, concurring in judgment); <em>id.</em>, at 71 (O'Connor, J., dissenting).  We have no reason to view the rule otherwise, and on this assumption, it does not violate due process, see <em>infra</em>, at 31-39.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Cf. <em>post</em>, at 3 (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., dissenting) ("The psychiatrist's explanation of Clark's condition was essential to understanding how he processes sensory data and therefore to deciding what information was in his mind at the time of the shooting.  Simply put, knowledge relies on cognition, and cognition can be affected by schizophrenia").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Though the term "diminished capacity" has been given different meanings, see, <em>e.g.</em>, Morse, Undiminished Confusion in Diminished Capacity, 75 J. Crim. L. &amp; C. 1 (1984) ("The diminished capacity doctrine allows a criminal defendant to introduce evidence of mental abnormality at trial either to negate a mental element of the crime charged, thereby exonerating the defendant of that charge, or to reduce the degree of crime for which the defendant may be convicted, even if the defendant's conduct satisfied all the formal elements of a higher offense"), California, a jurisdiction with which the concept has traditionally been associated, understood it to be simply a "&nbsp;'showing that the defendant's mental capacity was reduced by mental illness, mental defect or intoxication,'&nbsp;" <em>People </em>v. <em>Berry</em>, 18 Cal. 3d 509, 517, 556 P.&nbsp;2d 777, 781 (1976) (in banc) (quoting <em>People </em>v. <em>Castillo</em>, 70 Cal. 2d 264, 270, 449 P.&nbsp;2d 449, 452 (1969); emphasis deleted), abrogated by Cal. Penal Code Ann. 25(a), 28(a)-(b), 29 (West 1999 and Supp. 2006).  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It is beyond question that Arizona may preclude such a defense, see <em>Fisher</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 328 U.&nbsp;S. 463, 466-476 (1946), and there is no doubt that the Arizona Legislature meant to do so, see Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. 13-502(A) (West 2001) ("Mental disease or defect does not include disorders that result from acute voluntary intoxication or withdrawal from alcohol or drugs, character defects, psychosexual disorders or impulse control disorders.  Conditions that do not constitute legal insanity include but are not limited to momentary, temporary conditions arising from the pressure of the circumstances, moral decadence, depravity or passion growing out of anger, jealousy, revenge, hatred or other motives in a person who does not suffer from a mental disease or defect or an abnormality that is manifested only by criminal conduct").  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Our observation about the impact of mental-disease evidence on understandings of capacity in no way undermines the assertion by the American Psychiatric Association, the American Psychological Association, and the American Academy of Psychiatry in this case that "[e]xpert evidence of mental disorders ... is ... relevant to the mental-state issues raised by <em>mens rea </em>requirements," Brief for American Psychiatric Association et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 15.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNopinion1.44" href="#FRopinion1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A State in which the burden of persuasion as to a defendant's sanity lies with the prosecution might also be justified in restricting mental-disease and capacity evidence to insanity determinations owing to the potential of mental-disease evidence to mislead and the risk of misjudgment inherent in capacity evidence.  We need not, in the context of this case, address that issue.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNopinion1.45" href="#FRopinion1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Arizona's rule is supported by a further practical reason, though not as weighty as those just considered.  As mentioned before, if substantial mental-disease and capacity evidence is accepted as rebutting <em>mens rea</em> in a given case, the affirmative defense of insanity will probably not be reached or ruled upon; the defendant will simply be acquitted (or perhaps convicted of a lesser included offense).  If an acquitted defendant suffers from a mental disease or defect that makes him dangerous, he will neither be confined nor treated psychiatrically unless a judge so orders after some independent commitment proceeding.  But if a defendant succeeds in showing himself insane, Arizona law (and presumably that of every other State with an insanity rule) will require commitment and treatment as a consequence of that finding without more.  It makes sense, then, to channel capacity evidence to the issue structured to deal with mental incapacity when such a claim is raised successfully.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Jones,</em> 463 U.&nbsp;S., at 368 ("The purpose of commitment following an insanity acquittal ... is to treat the individual's mental illness and protect him and society from his potential dangerousness").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNopinion1.46" href="#FRopinion1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The rule also deals in a practical way with those whose insanity has been shown to make them dangerous to others.  See n. 45, <em>supra</em>.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> CLARK v. ARIZONA [05-5966], 548 U.S. 735 (2006)</h2>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Citation:</b> 548 U.S. 735</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 05-5966</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> April 19, 2006</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 29, 2006</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);

        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
